# 🚀 DeepEval Evaluation Results

## ✅ PASS - test_case_0

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How does offline eval check correctness, while online eval tracks production normalcy via score distribution?
- **Actual Output:** - Offline eval checks correctness by running the system on a fixed golden dataset with an answer key (human labels). You compare the system’s outputs to the gold answers (e.g., UPSC grader scores vs. human scores) and gate deployment based on those comparisons. It’s fast, cheap, repeatable, and intended to verify the application is working correctly before launch.

- Online eval checks production normalcy because it runs on live traffic without a golden answer key. Instead of measuring absolute correctness, it monitors signals and distributions (for example, the distribution of scores the grader produces over time) against a baseline. If the live distribution shifts (sudden spike in high scores, changes in latency, emergent bias, etc.), that signals abnormal behavior or drift and triggers investigation. Online eval often uses sampling to reduce cost (e.g., monitor 1,000 of 50,000 conversations) and can also use heuristics (like checking faithfulness to retrieved context) to estimate quality without gold answers.

- Key point: offline = correctness (pre-deploy, compare to gold); online = normalcy (post-deploy, detect drift/anomalies on live traffic). Both are complementary.
- **Expected Output:** Offline eval checks correctness by comparing the system’s outputs to human-labeled answers or a gold dataset. Online eval tracks normalcy in production by monitoring signals like the score distribution over time and comparing it to a baseline; if the distribution shifts suddenly, it may indicate abnormal behavior.

</details>

## ✅ PASS - test_case_1

- **Input:** Does offline eval check correctness, while online eval monitors production normalcy and drift?
- **Actual Output:** Yes.

Offline eval checks correctness — done before deployment using a fixed golden dataset/answer key to gate releases and catch regressions.  
Online eval monitors production normalcy — runs on live traffic without a golden answer key to detect drift, emergent failures, latency issues, etc., often using sampling to control cost.
- **Expected Output:** Yes. Offline eval checks whether the system is **correct** against known answers, while online eval checks whether the system is **running normally in production** by monitoring things like score distribution, drift, feedback, cost, and speed.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_2

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do online score distributions compare with offline human-mark agreement?
- **Actual Output:** - Offline (human-mark agreement): measures correctness — you directly compare the model’s scores to human/golden marks (e.g., UPSC grader: system marks should be close to human marks). This is a direct correctness metric obtained during offline evaluation.

- Online (score distributions): measures normalcy in production, not direct correctness. You collect model scores over time, build a baseline distribution (e.g., weekly distribution of student scores), and compare current distributions to that baseline. Large shifts (e.g., suddenly many high scores) trigger investigation because they indicate the system is behaving abnormally.

- Key differences and caveats:
  - Online distributions are a proxy signal — they can flag drift or failures but don’t prove correctness (you lack the human/golden answers in production).
  - Distribution shifts might be due to real-world input changes (not only model faults), so they require follow-up investigation.
  - When ground truth is unavailable, use proxy signals in online eval (user thumbs up/down, faithfulness checks, other metrics that don’t need the correct answer) and sampling to reduce cost.
- **Expected Output:** Online score distributions do **not directly measure correctness** like offline human-mark agreement does.

- **Offline evaluation** compares system scores against **human scores**, so it checks **correctness**.
- **Online evaluation** tracks the **distribution of scores in production** to see whether the system is **running normally** or has drifted.

So if the online score distribution changes a lot from the baseline, it suggests something abnormal, but it **does not guarantee** the scores still match human judgment.

</details>

Sentences 2 and 3 are also supported by nodes 1 and 3, and sentence 4 is supported by nodes 2 and 3, which note that a changed online distribution can indicate abnormality but does not guarantee human-level correctness. Since every sentence in the expected output is attributed to retrieval context, the recall is perfect. |

There are no irrelevant nodes ranked above relevant ones, so nothing needs to be pushed lower. ## ❌ FAIL - test_case_3

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How does synthetic data bootstrap eval datasets with scarce representative examples?
- **Actual Output:** - Because you don’t yet have enough human examples, you use an LLM to generate candidate evaluation examples from your application context (e.g., create question–answer pairs from your RAG documents).  
- This synthetic generation does the bulk of the work quickly (DeepEval recommends an "80/20" split: ~80% by the LLM, ~20% manual review).  
- Practically: generate a small, high-quality set (recommend ~50, up to 100 for complex apps), then manually review and prune so the final “golden” set is concise and accurate.  
- Ground the synthetic examples in real product behavior or available documents (prefer human-reviewed examples first, then production/historical data if available) so the eval is meaningful.  
- Use LLM-as-judge techniques to scale and reproduce scoring and to get diagnostic reasons for failures.  
- Caveat: synthetic data is easy to overproduce and abuse—use sparingly, focus on quality, and rely on careful manual vetting to ensure representativeness.
- **Expected Output:** Synthetic data helps **bootstrap evaluation datasets** when you **don’t have enough representative examples** by generating context-grounded test cases from your knowledge base. In DeepEval, this is useful for RAG apps where manual dataset creation is hard, and it lets you create evaluation data even when real examples are scarce.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_4

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do DeepEval's synthetic datasets, LLM-judge, and component evals improve RAG testing?
- **Actual Output:** Synthetic datasets
- DeepEval can generate synthetic documents from your knowledge base (grounded in your context), so you don't need a preexisting labeled dataset.  
- It can produce single-turn and multi-turn examples and edge cases that are hard to collect manually, increasing coverage of likely failure modes.  
- Because RAG evaluation needs linked retrieval contexts + generated responses, synthetic data lets you create realistic context–response pairs tailored to retriever and generator behavior.

LLM-as-judge
- Lets an LLM score outputs using natural-language prompts instead of hand-coded formulas, making it easy to build custom metrics and get human-like judgments at scale.  
- Scoring is scalable and (to an extent) reproducible: DeepEval provides built-in metrics with carefully tuned system prompts so you don’t have to craft them from scratch.  
- The judge also returns reasons for scores, which helps pinpoint where the RAG app is lacking.

Component-level evals
- You can run component (not just end-to-end) evaluations to isolate retriever vs generator failures — important because injection-phase components typically show up in retriever scores and downstream effects differ by component.  
- Tracing and component evals let you attribute errors to specific parts of the RAG pipeline and measure component-level metrics rather than only pipeline-level outcomes.

Caveats and practical mitigations (from context)
- Self-enhancement bias: a model evaluating its own outputs may overrate them. Mitigate by using a stronger/larger judge model than the one under test.  
- Judge errors and prompt sensitivity: judges can be wrong or sensitive to wording. Mitigate by increasing dataset coverage, running multiple evaluations and averaging, and using DeepEval’s vetted built-in metrics/prompts.  
- Cost: LLM-based judging incurs API costs; keep datasets focused (quality over quantity) and carefully select test questions.

Overall effect on RAG testing
- Combining grounded synthetic datasets, LLM-judge scoring, and component-level evals gives realistic, targeted test cases, scalable human-like scoring with explanations, and the ability to pinpoint whether failures come from retriever or generator — improving detection, diagnosis, and iteration speed for RAG systems.
- **Expected Output:** DeepEval improves RAG testing by making evaluation **scalable, reproducible, and explainable**.  

- **Synthetic datasets** help you generate evaluation data when you don’t have enough real examples, especially for RAG workflows where context and response are linked.
- **LLM-as-a-judge** lets you use an LLM to score outputs with reasons for each metric, so you can understand **where the application is lacking**.
- **Component evals** let you test parts of the RAG pipeline separately, like the **retriever** and the **generator**, instead of treating the whole system as a black box.

This makes it easier to test failure modes, reproduce updates, and evaluate RAG applications more effectively.

</details>

## ✅ PASS - test_case_5

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Compare LLM-as-judge vs manual eval: scalability, reproducibility, and metric-level reasons.
- **Actual Output:** Scalability
- LLM-as-judge: Highly scalable and fast — can run many evaluations in minutes and call many APIs in parallel. However cost grows with number of metrics × examples (API/token cost). Suitable for larger datasets where humans can’t keep up.
- Manual eval: Not scalable. Humans need rest, pay, can be sick, and cannot evaluate high volumes quickly. Best suited for small, careful datasets or gold-standard checks.

Reproducibility
- LLM-as-judge: Lower reproducibility risks due to prompt sensitivity and model stochasticity. Small changes in the system prompt or judge instructions can shift scores significantly. Also self-enhancement bias (especially if the same model generates and judges) and judge errors (hallucination, reasoning mistakes) reduce consistent repeatability. You can improve reproducibility by using a more powerful judge model (larger than the app model), using well-crafted system prompts (e.g., deep eval built-ins), increasing dataset coverage, and running multiple evaluations and averaging results.
- Manual eval: Also imperfect — humans bring bias, inconsistency, and fatigue, so single-human judgments are not perfectly reproducible. But humans are better at nuanced, subtle judgments and catching errors that an LLM judge might miss. Reproducibility can be improved with clear annotation guidelines and multiple annotators/aggregation, though this raises cost and time.

Metric-level reasons (why one method favors certain metrics)
- LLM-as-judge:
  - Good for metrics that need semantic/intent-level comparison (coverage, clarity, conversational quality) because LLMs understand language patterns and can compare non-deterministic outputs beyond word-for-word matching.
  - Works well when programmatic checks fail (e.g., RAG or free-text responses).
  - Vulnerable for factual correctness and subtle nuance if the judge hallucinaties or is biased; prompt wording and judge model capability strongly influence scores.
  - Cost multiplies with number of metrics (each metric often requires an API call), so evaluating many metrics across many examples becomes expensive.
- Manual eval:
  - Strongest for factual correctness, nuance, and catching reasoning/hallucination errors — human judgment remains the gold standard.
  - Expensive and slow for many metrics or large datasets, so often limited in coverage (fewer examples/metrics).
  - Programmatic metrics (latency, P95, cost per query, availability, token usage, drift/regression) are generally measured operationally rather than via human judgment; humans aren’t needed for these.

Practical guidance from context
- Use humans for high-quality gold-standard judgments but accept limited scale.
- Use LLM-as-judge for scalable, semantic comparisons, but mitigate bias/errors by: choosing a more powerful judge than the app model, using vetted system prompts (e.g., deep eval built-ins), increasing dataset coverage, and averaging multiple runs. Avoid using the same model to generate and judge.
- **Expected Output:** LLM-as-judge is better for **scalability** and **reproducibility** than manual eval. It lets you evaluate at scale, reproduce small application updates, and get a **reason for each metric**, which helps identify where the application is lacking. Manual eval is useful, but synthetic/LLM-based evaluation is more practical when you don’t have enough representative examples.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_6

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** What are the 3 eval approaches, and which one reduces human bias most?
- **Actual Output:** The three main eval approaches in the context are:
- Human judgment (humans score/evaluate directly)
- Automated checks / programmatic evaluation
- LLM-as-a-judge (model evaluates outputs like a human)

(There is also a fourth approach mentioned: production feedback / A/B testing after deployment.)

Which reduces human bias most?
- Automated checks reduce human bias the most because they are programmatic and objective (no individual human raters). LLM-as-a-judge reduces rater variability compared to humans but can introduce model-related biases, and human judgment is the most subject to individual bias.
- **Expected Output:** The 3 eval approaches are:

1. **Manual creation**  
2. **Historical data**  
3. **Synthetic data**

**Synthetic data** reduces human bias the most because it is automated and does not depend on humans, so it avoids bias, fatigue, illness, and scaling issues.

</details>

### Metrics

| Status | Metric | Score | Threshold | Reason |
|:---:|:---|:---:|:---:|:---|

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_7

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** What manual-question-paper issues arise, and how do coverage, balance, cost, scaling, and updates affect them?
- **Actual Output:** Issues that arise with manually created question papers
- Knowledge/coverage risk: the human author must know what’s in the knowledge base (every page/section) or else the paper will miss important areas.
- Poor coverage: if coverage is low you won’t test many aspects of the KB; gaps mean the eval is not representative.
- Imbalanced difficulty: overly hard papers will fail worthy students; too lenient papers won’t differentiate performance.
- Topic skew / bias: teachers bring their own biases (e.g., a physics teacher favoring physics/chemistry problems), producing uneven topic distribution.
- Human factors: availability, mood, fatigue, sickness can reduce question quality or consistency.
- Cost: creating and maintaining manual papers requires paid human resources (multiple teachers), making it expensive.
- Update burden: when the KB changes (chapters cut/edited), teachers must re-read and revise papers — the update cycle is hard to manage.
- Poor scalability: producing many exams/subjects or frequent re‑runs multiplies manual effort beyond practical limits.

How coverage, balance, cost, scaling, and updates affect these issues
- Coverage: insufficient knowledge of the KB or low coverage in the paper leads to blindspots in evaluation; ensuring full coverage requires more time and expertise from humans.
- Balance (coverage × difficulty mix): you must intentionally mix topics (theory/practical, aptitude/reasoning) and tune difficulty probabilistically; failure to balance produces biased or uninformative evaluations (false negatives/positives).
- Cost: hiring multiple skilled authors/reviewers to guarantee coverage and balance raises expenses; cost limits how many papers or revisions you can afford.
- Scaling: manual processes don’t scale — increasing subjects, exam frequency, or user volumes requires proportionally more human labor, making consistent quality hard to sustain.
- Updates: any KB change forces manual rework; frequent updates become infeasible to track and revalidate by hand, causing the eval to drift out of sync with current content.
- **Expected Output:** Manual question-paper creation has several issues:

- **Coverage:** the setter must know the full knowledge base well, or important topics may be missed.
- **Balance:** questions must be balanced in difficulty and topic mix; too hard, too easy, or too focused on one area makes testing weak.
- **Bias:** human setters may favor certain subjects or types of questions.
- **Cost:** it requires manual labor, so it becomes expensive.
- **Scaling:** it is hard to create many papers or handle many subjects with manual effort.
- **Updates:** if the knowledge base changes, the paper must be revised manually, which is difficult to manage.

So, manual creation is slow, costly, hard to scale, and vulnerable to bias and outdated content.

</details>

Great recall. |

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_8

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Compare synthetic, manual, and historical methods for eval setup, emphasizing coverage and scaling.
- **Actual Output:** Manual
- Coverage: Potentially highest and most representative when created carefully — you control topic selection and edge cases. But human bias can reduce coverage diversity if not managed.
- Scaling: Poor. Manual creation and review are expensive, slow, and hard to scale across many documents/subjects or frequent updates.
- Other notes: Best-quality ("golden") data comes from manual effort; recommended where feasible.

Historical (production) data
- Coverage: Reflects real user behavior, so grounded in product usage, but coverage can be narrow — it only represents the types of users and queries seen in production (may miss rare cases or new scenarios).
- Scaling: Easier to collect at scale (logs), but producing correct answer keys often requires manual labeling, so evaluation cost/effort remains significant.
- Other notes: Can reveal real failures but you may only discover issues after productionizing the app.

Synthetic
- Coverage: Can be designed to cover many cases and large document sets (including updated knowledge bases), and can be automated to target gaps that manual/historical data miss. However, quality and true representativeness depend on generation approach; synthetic material can be biased or low-quality if abused.
- Scaling: Very good — automated generation scales easily and can be re-run after KB updates. Cheaper and faster than manual.
- Quality-control requirements: Deep Eval recommends an 80/20 workflow — let the LLM do ~80% of generation, then perform ~20% careful manual review. Keep synthetic eval sets small and high-quality (recommended ~50, up to 100 Q/A pairs), and prune anything not meeting gold quality.

Practical recommendation (from context)
- Prefer human-reviewed examples first, then production/historical data, and use synthetic only to fill gaps or bootstrap when manual/historical data are insufficient.
- Use synthetic sparingly and carefully: generate targeted, grounded examples, then manually review and keep a compact golden set to ensure coverage without sacrificing quality.
- **Expected Output:** Synthetic data is the best for eval setup when you want high coverage and scaling. It is automated, low cost, avoids human bias and fatigue, and can regenerate the dataset whenever the knowledge base changes.

Manual creation gives good control but has major limits: it is costly, slow, hard to scale, and depends on human availability and expertise. Historical data is easier than manual, but it may have limited coverage and still needs correct answers to score properly.

So, for a RAG evaluation setup, synthetic > historical > manual in terms of scaling, while manual can still be useful for expert, high-quality questions.

</details>

## ✅ PASS - test_case_11

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Why prioritize LLM evals now, and how do they improve interview edge and product thinking?
- **Actual Output:** Why prioritize LLM evals now
- High industry demand: every company wants more LLM-based apps, so specialization in building and testing them is increasingly valuable.
- Scarcity of expertise and resources: most students learn LangChain, RAG, agents, prompts, etc., but few focus on LLM evals—so there’s little competition.
- Practical importance: evaluating LLMs answers product-critical questions (e.g., “Can this model be used for this task?”, “Is it good enough to ship?”, “Is the RAG answer grounded?”, “Is the chatbot safe?”, “Is latency acceptable?”).

How LLM evals give you an interview edge
- Interviewers frequently ask “How would you evaluate a RAG application or an agent?” Many candidates can’t answer. Knowing LLM evals lets you answer these practical questions clearly.
- You can demonstrate knowledge of the entire testing setup (not just a metric): test design, what to measure, when to test, and which tools to use—showing deeper, production-oriented thinking than most peers.

How LLM evals shift you to product thinking
- From portfolio to product: instead of building projects just to show, you start asking “Will this work for crores of users?” and “Can I bring this to production?”
- You learn to evaluate multidimensional quality (not just correctness): relevance, completeness, grounding, safety, latency, robustness, prompt/version comparisons, etc.
- You adopt systematic, repeatable testing practices for probabilistic systems (model vs application evals), so design, deployment and monitoring decisions reflect real-world scale and risk.

In short: LLM evals are timely because demand is high and expertise is rare; they boost interview performance by covering questions many candidates can’t answer; and they mature your mindset from hobby projects to product-ready, production-thinking engineering.
- **Expected Output:** LLM evals matter now because LLM-based applications are becoming widely used, and companies need to know whether these products actually work well in production. They also help you stay ahead in interviews, since questions like “How will you evaluate a RAG application or an agent?” are common, and many candidates can’t answer them properly.

They also improve product thinking: instead of building projects just for a portfolio, you start thinking like a company and ask whether the application will work reliably for many users, in production, at scale.

</details>

### Metrics

| Status | Metric | Score | Threshold | Reason |
|:---:|:---|:---:|:---:|:---|
| ✅ | **Contextual Recall** | 1.00 | 0.50 | The score is 1.00 because sentence 1 is fully supported by node 1 in retrieval context (LLM-based applications are widely used and companies need to know if they work in production), sentence 2 is also fully supported by node 1 (they help with interviews and thinking about whether an app works at scale in production), and sentence 3 is likewise fully supported by node 1 (it shifts your mindset toward company-level product thinking and reliability for many users). |
| ✅ | **Contextual Precision** | 1.00 | 0.50 | The score is 1.00 because the top-ranked retrieval contexts are all relevant node in retrieval contexts and they align tightly with the input: node 1 supports why LLM evals matter now with "each and every company today wants... more and more LLM based applications" and "LLM evals is basically a study of evaluating LLM based applications," node 2 connects them to interview edge with "you can perform better in the interview in comparison to your competitors" and "How will you evaluate a rag application? How will you evaluate an agent?," and node 3 reinforces product thinking with "will it work for crores of people? Can I bring it to production?" Since there are no irrelevant nodes in retrieval contexts ranked above relevant ones, the score cannot be higher. |

## ✅ PASS - test_case_13

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Compare experiment tracking and pipeline reproducibility in LLMOps vs MLOps.
- **Actual Output:** High-level summary
- Both MLops and LLMOps use pipelines to achieve reproducibility: you design workflows so providing the same inputs triggers the same end-to-end process and produces versioned artifacts (data, models).
- But what needs to be tracked and how reproduction is enforced differs a lot because ML outputs are simple/deterministic (numbers) while LLM outputs are complex, dynamic text and streaming.

Side-by-side comparison

1) Purpose of pipelines / reproducibility
- MLOps: pipelines (data pipeline + model pipeline) make training/evaluation/tuning reproducible across runs. You version raw → cleaned → feature data, and model artifacts (via model registry). Reproduction typically means rerunning the data + model pipeline (retrain) on a dataset/version.
- LLMOps: pipelines are workflow-oriented (retrieval → augmentation → generation) triggered per user query. Reproducibility means reproducing the whole workflow and the journey of each input through retrieval/augmentation/generation, not just a single training run.

2) What experiment tracking records
- MLOps: track data versions, training hyperparameters, model versions, evaluation metrics (accuracy, precision, recall, F1). Track transformations so you can go from B → A (artifact lineage).
- LLMOps: track similar versioning but also many extra workflow details: which documents were used, chunking strategy (chunk size, overlap), embedding model and its provider, output dimensions, prompt/templates, retrieval parameters, etc. You must “lock” these internals because outputs vary even for the same input.

3) Complexity of evaluations and their role in reproducibility
- MLOps: evaluations are relatively simple (compare prediction vs ground truth per row; compute scalar metrics). Easier to reason about and reproduce.
- LLMOps: evaluations are complex and heavyweight because outputs are text and non-deterministic. Evaluation itself is a deep component of the pipeline and affects how you reproduce/accept behavior.

4) Tracing vs logging/experiment tracking
- MLOps: logging and experiment tracking are standard; tracing the token-level journey is generally not required.
- LLMOps: in addition to logging/experiment tracking you need tracing—recording the full journey of every input through the workflow (retrieval hits, augmentation steps, prompt used, generation tokens). Tracing is an added/critical step to reproduce and debug outputs.

5) Guardrails and production behavior
- MLOps: outputs are numeric/statistical; there’s less need for runtime guardrails to prevent toxicity or hallucination.
- LLMOps: you must add guardrails (safety checks, constraints) and track them; these affect reproducibility because they change runtime behavior and must be versioned/tracked.

6) Post-production differences
- MLOps: retraining loop is common — detect data drift, rerun pipelines on new data to restore intended behavior.
- LLMOps: retraining is less commonly used; instead you monitor tracing/metrics and fix application components (retrieval, prompts, guardrails) and version those fixes. Reproduction often requires reapplying those fixes and reproducing the traced run.

Practical implication (from context)
- For MLOps: focus on data + model versioning, experiment logs of hyperparams and metrics, and retraining pipelines to reproduce results.
- For LLMOps: you need richer experiment tracking (documents, chunking, embeddings, prompts, providers), full tracing of input→output flows, and versioned guardrails and workflow components to reproduce and debug nondeterministic textual outputs.
- **Expected Output:** Experiment tracking and pipeline reproducibility are similar in both, but they work differently.

- **Experiment tracking** means recording what was used in a run:
  - In **LLMOps/RAG**, track documents, chunk size, chunk overlap, embedding model, provider, output dimensions, etc.
  - It helps **lock the pipeline** so you can understand why a certain output came.

- **Pipeline reproducibility** means making the workflow repeatable end to end:
  - In **MLOps**, you build pipelines so when data is provided, the system can automatically train, evaluate, and tune.
  - In **LLMOps**, the pipeline is more of a workflow like **retrieval → augmentation → generation**, and every query triggers that workflow.

**Main difference:**
- **MLOps** reproducibility is about repeating a more fixed training/evaluation pipeline.
- **LLMOps** reproducibility is about tracking a more dynamic, multi-step application pipeline where outputs depend on moving parts like the retriever, vector DB, and LLM.

</details>

### Metrics

|:---:|:---|:---:|:---:|:---|

There are no irrelevant nodes ranked above relevant ones, so the precision is maximal. |

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_14

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Why is LLM Ops eval harder than ML, given dynamic text outputs?
- **Actual Output:** Because LLM outputs are fundamentally different from traditional ML predictions. Key reasons from the discussion:

- Non-deterministic/probabilistic outputs: the same input can produce different, varied outputs each time, so you cannot rely on a fixed label per example or simple repeated evaluation to get the same result.  
- Variable, complex outputs: responses can be a word, sentence, paragraph or more, so mapping them to a single ground-truth label (or doing keyword matching) fails; multiple different outputs can all be correct.  
- Multi-dimensional quality requirements: you must judge correctness, relevance, completeness, groundedness (does it cite the retrieval source?), safety, etc., not just “right or wrong.”  
- Moving parts and workflows: LLM apps often include retrievers, vector DBs and API-hosted models; these components and the orchestration workflow introduce additional sources of variability.  
- Dynamic cost and resource usage: input and output lengths vary, so computation, latency and cost change per request, complicating evaluation and budgeting.  
- No single fixed metric: unlike ML formulas (accuracy, precision, MSE), LLM evaluation needs task-specific metrics and often human judgment, making evaluation harder and less straightforward.
- **Expected Output:** LLM Ops eval is harder because the output is **text, not a fixed label or number**, and it is **dynamic/probabilistic**. For the same input, an LLM can give different valid outputs, so you can’t just compare prediction vs actual like in ML classification.

Also, in LLM apps there are **many moving parts**—LLM, retriever, vector DB, prompt, retrieved context—so evaluation must account for the whole workflow, not just one model output.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_18

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Does standalone retriever/generator success guarantee RAC success, or is workflow-level eval still needed?
- **Actual Output:** No — workflow-level eval is still needed.

Why:
- Component success can hide bad interactions: retriever may include the correct doc within K, but generator prioritizes earlier (incorrect) docs due to ordering or system-prompt bias, producing a wrong answer.
- Missing or misordered re-ranking can cause the correct document to be deprioritized even though retriever and generator individually pass their checks.
- Other pipeline issues (query rewriter, embeddings, reranker, prompt ordering) can produce failures only visible when components interact.
- Application-level failures (e.g., end-to-end latency > threshold) aren’t caught by component or workflow evals unless you add an app-level eval.

Conclusion: keep component-level evals, add workflow-level evals to check interactions, and add application-level evals (like latency) to cover remaining failure modes.
- **Expected Output:** No. Standalone retriever and generator success does not guarantee RAC success.

Even if the retriever and generator work properly on their own, the workflow can still fail because:
- the retriever may return the right document but in the wrong order,
- the generator may use the wrong context from the retrieved documents,
- the combined retriever-generator behavior may produce a wrong final answer.

So, workflow-level evaluation is still needed to check how the components work together.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_19

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Why can a RAG app fail despite working retriever, generator, pipeline evals, and low latency?
- **Actual Output:** No — it can still fail. Even if retriever, generator, their combined workflow evals pass and latency is fine, other failure points remain:

- Ingestion / embedding mismatch or errors: the query→vector step is carried over from ingestion. If embeddings or ingestion were wrong, out-of-date, or misconfigured, the pipeline can still return bad context.  
- Missing or misconfigured intermediate components: re-rankers, query-rewriters, augmentation steps, system prompt, etc., can cause wrong priorities or bad prompts even when retriever/generator checks pass. (Example in context: correct doc sits at lowest priority until a re-ranker fixes order.)  
- Uncontrolled user queries / distribution shift: evals may not cover real-world, ambiguous, adversarial, or out-of-distribution queries, so behavior can break in production.  
- Nondeterminism and edge cases: LLM outputs are probabilistic; intermittent or rare failures may not show up in tests.  
- Eval blind spots / insufficient coverage: component and workflow evals can miss user-facing criteria (groundedness types, edge scenarios, formatting, safety), so passing them doesn’t ensure correctness for all real queries.  
- Application-level requirements beyond latency: UX, readability, correctness for business rules, freshness of answers, and other service-level objectives must be evaluated separately.

You need additional application-level evals and monitoring (covering coverage, freshness, priority, safety/usability, and real-traffic checks) to gain stronger guarantees.
- **Expected Output:** A RAG app can still fail because even if the retriever, generator, and their combined pipeline evals pass, the **end-to-end application** may still have issues not covered by those checks. For example, a component may return the right documents individually, but the overall system can still produce a wrong answer due to **workflow-level interaction problems**, or fail at the **application level** if it doesn’t meet practical constraints like latency, user experience, or deployment requirements.

</details>

## ✅ PASS - test_case_20

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Compare workflow-level vs app-level evals for retriever-generator errors and latency.
- **Actual Output:** Workflow-level eval (retriever + generator) vs app-level eval — comparison focused on retriever-generator errors and latency:

Purpose
- Workflow-level eval: checks the interaction between retriever and generator — whether the retrieved context and the generator together produce correct, grounded answers. It flags mistakes that only appear when components combine (e.g., wrong document ordering causes wrong answer).
- App-level eval: checks user-facing, deployment and operational properties of the whole application (latency, throughput, UX constraints, SLA). It verifies the app is usable in the real-world, not just “correct” logically.

What each monitors / failure modes caught
- Workflow-level:
  - Mis-prioritized documents across components (example: correct doc D5 was lowest priority → generator used wrong docs).
  - Cases where retriever and generator individually pass component tests but their combination produces wrong or ungrounded outputs.
  - Need for re-ranking or re-ordering of retrieved docs (remediation: add a re-ranker to promote the correct doc).
  - Groundedness/faithfulness: generator hallucination despite correct context can be detected when comparing context → answer.
- App-level:
  - Latency (example in context: whole pipeline returns answer in 10s → unacceptable UX/deployability).
  - End-to-end user experience and operational constraints (response time thresholds, availability).
  - Effects of upstream issues visible to users (e.g., slow data ingestion showing up as slow or stale retrieval).
  - Anything outside retriever+generator logic: input variability (user-controlled), chunking impacts on UX, resource/cost/throughput constraints.

What they do not guarantee
- Workflow-level eval alone does not guarantee the app is deployable or meets user needs (it won’t catch high latency or availability).
- Component-level + workflow-level passing does not guarantee app-level requirements are met.
- Even with retriever eval, generator eval, and workflow eval passing, problems can still occur at app-level (latency, UX, infra), so you need app-level evals too.

Recommended metrics / checks (from context)
- Workflow-level: fidelity/faithfulness checks that answer is grounded in retrieved docs; document-priority checks (would flag needing a re-ranker); combined retrieval+generation correctness metrics.
- App-level: latency thresholds (end-to-end response time), deployment readiness, user wait-time constraints, throughput/SLA checks.
- Keep component-level evals (retriever-specific metrics, generator-specific metrics such as augmentation eval/format/grounding) alongside workflow and app evals.

Concrete example (from context)
- Problem: retriever returned docs with correct doc at lowest priority → generator produced wrong answer.
  - Workflow eval would flag this combination failure.
  - Remediation: add re-ranker to raise the correct doc (D5) so D1–D4 drop and pipeline produces correct answers.
- Remaining problem: after fixing above, end-to-end latency is 10s → app still not deployable.
  - App-level eval with latency threshold would flag this; action could be optimization, caching, model/infra changes.

Bottom line
- Workflow-level evals are necessary to catch interaction errors between retriever and generator (e.g., wrong doc priorities, combined hallucinations), but they do not replace app-level evals.
- You must add app-level evals (latency, user-facing constraints, operational metrics) to ensure the RAG application is actually usable and deployable.
- **Expected Output:** Workflow-level evals check whether the retriever and generator work well together, so they can catch errors like the retriever returning the right document but in the wrong priority, causing the generator to produce the wrong answer.

App-level evals go one step higher and check the whole user-facing application, including things like latency. Even if retriever, generator, and their combined workflow all pass, the app can still fail if, for example, it takes too long to respond.

</details>

Great recall! |

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_21

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do multiple Qs/topic from different teachers increase coverage and reduce bias?
- **Actual Output:** - Diversity of perspectives: different subject teachers (e.g., biology vs physics) naturally propose questions from different parts of NCERT. Pooling many teachers’ questions brings in topics across the whole book instead of one teacher’s narrow focus.

- Reduces individual bias: one teacher tends to over-represent their subject (biology teacher pushing more biology questions, physics teacher pushing physics). Mixing questions from multiple teachers prevents any single teacher’s preference from dominating, producing a more equal proportion across topics.

- Larger candidate set increases coverage: asking multiple teachers to produce more questions (example in context: 3 teachers × 30 questions = 90) gives a wider set of possible topics to choose from. From that larger set you can select 15 that maximize topical coverage across chapters.

- Filtering and chunking avoid repetition: teachers (or an LLM critic) filter pages, then group similar pages into chunks so overlapping content is merged rather than producing redundant questions. That lets selected questions span distinct contexts instead of repeating the same theme.

- Quality control via critique and selection: an expert (or LLM critique) evaluates and selects the best 15 from the pooled 90 according to criteria (coverage, non-bias, quality). That selection enforces equality and relevance to NCERT.

- Tuning/evolution ensures multidimensional assessment: after selection the questions are “tuned” (made multi-aspect, different complexity) so the final paper tests reasoning, aptitude, factual recall and presentation—helping assess students across multiple dimensions rather than a single teacher’s favored skill.

- Trade-off acknowledged: this approach uses more time and resources (preparing 90 vs. 15) but yields a less biased, higher-coverage, and more effective question paper.
- **Expected Output:** By generating multiple questions per topic from different teachers, you cover different parts and angles of the same context, so the overall coverage increases. It also reduces bias because you’re not relying on one teacher’s style or interpretation—different teachers create different question types, making the final set more balanced.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_22

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** What LLM-eval dataset issues do filtering, multi-teacher questions, and evolution solve?
- **Actual Output:** - Filtering
  - Fixes low-quality/noisy synthetic items by removing questions that are ambiguous or incomplete.  
  - Enforces self-containment and clarity so each query is independently answerable from the context (reduces noise and need for manual review).  
  - Improves reproducibility and consistency by applying a deterministic pass/fail threshold and a strong LLM judge.

- Multi‑teacher / multiple‑candidate generation (e.g., generate 10 → select best 2)
  - Increases coverage and diversity of question forms and phrasings (mitigates limited coverage and narrow distribution from historical/manual data).  
  - Reduces single‑model bias and generation errors by producing many candidates and letting selection/judging pick the best.  
  - Cuts reliance on a single human author (less human bias, less tedious manual creation).

- Evolution (making questions progressively trickier)
  - Eliminates overly trivial, extractive Q/A pairs so evaluations don’t reward simple transcript lookup.  
  - Creates controlled difficulty levels and borderline cases (near 0.5 decision boundary) so you can distinguish real improvements between iterations/components.  
  - Lets you tune difficulty to avoid tests that are too easy (no discrimination) or too hard (good systems fail), improving sensitivity and meaningfulness of comparisons.
- **Expected Output:** They help create a better evaluation dataset:

- **Filtering** removes too-easy or direct questions, so only useful ones remain.
- **Multi-teacher questions** reduce bias by generating different questions from different parts of the context.
- **Evolution** turns raw questions into trickier ones that test reasoning, aptitude, and board-exam-level understanding.

Overall, this increases **coverage**, reduces **bias**, and makes the final questions more suitable for evaluating an LLM application.

</details>

There are no irrelevant nodes above any relevant ones, so nothing is ranked too high, which is why the score cannot be higher than perfect. |

## ✅ PASS - test_case_24

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do systematic, repeatable, criteria-based LLM evals differ from deterministic software tests?
- **Actual Output:** - Core definition
  - Software tests: verify deterministic logic against a single expected output (fixed input → fixed output).
  - LLM evals: systematic, repeatable tests judged against clear, pre-defined criteria to assess probabilistic behavior.

- Determinism vs probabilism
  - Software: same input always yields the same output (e.g., 2+2 = 4). Tests are binary pass/fail.
  - LLMs: same input can produce different, yet correct, outputs across runs/users/times. A single correct “expected” output is insufficient.

- What you measure
  - Software tests: mainly correctness (does the program produce the exact expected result).
  - LLM evals: multi-dimensional quality testing — correctness, relevance, completeness, grounding (is it sourced from intended documents, e.g., RAG), safety (no abusive/unsafe output), latency, cost (tokens/compute), and adherence to desired style.

- How you test
  - Software: write test cases with explicit expected outputs; run and check pass/fail.
  - LLMs: define rubrics/criteria and repeatable procedures to evaluate many aspects; acceptance is often probabilistic or threshold-based rather than absolute. “Vibe testing” (ad hoc few queries) is insufficient.

- Risks and examples (from context)
  - A chatbot hallucinating fabricated legal cases caused real harm — shows why grounding and careful evaluation matter.
  - For a CampusX RAG chatbot you must check not just correctness but whether answers come from your videos, are safe, timely, cost-effective, and match your explanation style.

- Takeaway
  - Deterministic software tests check a single truth. LLM evals require systematic, repeatable, criteria-driven assessment across multiple axes to handle variability and real-world risks.
- **Expected Output:** LLM evals are different because they test **probabilistic systems** with **multiple criteria**, not just one correct answer.

- **Systematic:** you use proper datasets with edge cases, not vibe testing.
- **Repeatable:** the same test set can be reused after changing prompt, model, retriever, or chunking.
- **Criteria-based:** you evaluate more than correctness, like safety, groundedness, simplicity, latency, and cost.

In contrast, **deterministic software tests** usually check whether the software gives the same correct output every time. For LLMs, the same input can produce different valid outputs, so evaluation is more complex.

</details>

## ✅ PASS - test_case_25

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Compare LLM evals vs metrics; include criteria, dataset, timing, and tools.
- **Actual Output:** LLM evals vs metrics — core difference
- Metrics = individual measurable quantities (accuracy, latency, P95, cost per query, precision/recall, availability, etc.). They are the numbers you report.
- LLM evals = the complete, systematic testing setup that uses a defined set of metrics (plus datasets, criteria, timing, methods and tools) to produce objective, reproducible evidence about an application's quality, reliability and correctness. In short: metrics are a component of LLM evals, not the whole.

Compare by required dimensions

- Criteria (what aspects you evaluate)
  - Metrics: represent single aspects (e.g., correctness, P95 latency, cost efficiency, toxicity score).  
  - LLM evals: define a full set of evaluation criteria (e.g., factual correctness, answer quality/explanation clarity, source grounding to course content, safety/toxicity, reliability/fallback behavior). The eval specifies which metrics map to each criterion and how to score/pass.

- Dataset (what you test on)
  - Metrics: reported values come from runs on some data, but metrics alone don’t define which inputs to use.
  - LLM evals: include curated datasets as a first-class artifact (test questions, edge cases, real user chats for production feedback). Datasets are designed to be systematic, cover edge cases, and enable repeatable comparisons across versions.

- Timing (when you run evaluations)
  - Metrics: can be measured at a moment in time (single run) and reported; timing context must be stated separately.
  - LLM evals: explicitly include timing decisions as part of the setup — offline testing, CI/regression tests, and production feedback (monitoring real user interactions after deployment). Repeatability is emphasized so you can compare version 1 vs version 2 on the same dataset and metrics.

- Tools & methods (how you evaluate)
  - Metrics: computed by code or monitoring systems (e.g., latency collectors, cost calculators, toxicity scorers). Metrics don’t prescribe the evaluation method.
  - LLM evals: specify the methods (human judgment, automated checks, LLM-as-judge, and production feedback) and the tooling used in the workflow (retrievers and RAG toolchains, observability/tracing systems, evaluation frameworks). Example: for a RAG chatbot you include the retriever and RAG tooling as part of the eval setup and choose methods (human or LLM-as-judge) appropriate to the criteria.

Practical implications
- Use metrics to quantify specific properties; use LLM evals to design repeatable, systematic tests that combine metrics, datasets, criteria, timing and tools so you can reliably decide whether changes improve or worsen the application.
- Example workflow elements in an LLM eval: define criteria (correctness, safety, grounding), build dataset (edge cases + sampled production chats), choose method (LLM-as-judge + spot human review), pick tools (retriever, RAG system, monitoring stack), run tests and compare metrics (accuracy, P95 latency, cost, drift) across versions.
- **Expected Output:** LLM evals are the **entire testing setup**, not just a metric.

- **Metrics** are just one part of evaluation, like accuracy or precision.
- **LLM evals** include:
  - **Criteria**: what you want to check, like correctness, safety, groundedness, latency
  - **Dataset**: the test cases used, often covering edge cases and real user chats
  - **Timing**: when you test, like offline or after deployment
  - **Tools**: what you use to run the evaluation, like Ragas for RAG systems

So, **metrics = one measure**, while **LLM evals = systematic, repeatable tests with clear criteria using a dataset and tools**.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_26

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do model evals and application evals compare in scope and purpose?
- **Actual Output:** Short answer: model evals test the model itself; application evals test the whole LLM-powered system. They differ in scope, purpose, and methodology.

Model evals
- Scope: the LLM (its capabilities/benchmarks, prompt variations, intrinsic behaviors).
- Purpose: measure model-level performance (benchmark scores, accuracy on standard tasks) to compare models or model versions.
- Typical output: model-centric metrics from fixed benchmark datasets.
- Note from context: these are simpler/standardized (often what people call “benchmarks”) and not the main focus here.

Application evals
- Scope: the entire LLM-based application/workflow (LLM + retriever, RAG pipeline, agent logic, UI, fallbacks, latency, safety controls).
- Purpose: answer practical questions about deployability and user safety — e.g., “Is this system good enough to ship?”, “Is RAG grounded?”, “Did prompt v2 improve the product?”, “Is latency acceptable?”, “Is the chatbot safe/reliable?”
- Methodology: systematic, repeatable testing using a defined set of metrics, test datasets (the “question paper”), and passing criteria; produces objective, reproducible evidence and supports regression testing when parts change.
- Why harder: multiple moving parts, non-deterministic behaviors, reliability and correctness (factuality, bias/toxicity), and interactions between components make evaluation more complex than simple model benchmarks.
- Important constraint: tests are run on fixed test datasets (not on arbitrary live user inputs) to ensure reproducibility and comparability.

(Disclaimer in context: “model eval” and “application eval” are informal labels used to simplify the distinction.)
- **Expected Output:** Model evals focus on evaluating the LLM itself, while application evals focus on evaluating LLM-based systems or applications. In scope, model evals are narrower and check the model’s performance directly; application evals are broader and include the whole setup like the retriever, prompt, chunking, safety, latency, and whether the answer is grounded in the right context. In purpose, both are systematic and repeatable tests, but application evals answer practical questions like “Is this good enough to ship?” or “Is the chatbot safe for users?”

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_27

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Why is observability needed to trace RAG input flow, latency, and cost?
- **Actual Output:** Because a RAG pipeline is a sequence of components whose outputs become the next component’s inputs, you need observability to understand, debug, and operate it in production. Specifically:

- To follow the input flow end-to-end: tracing shows which components the input went into, what each component returned, and how that output fed the next step—so you can locate where a wrong answer or unexpected behavior originated.
- To measure and attribute latency: tracing captures LLM and non‑LLM calls (retrievals, embeddings, etc.), letting you break down latency per component and aggregate it over windows (e.g., last 1h) to detect system‑level regressions rather than one-off spikes.
- To attribute and control cost: observability records LLM call counts, token usage, and other cost drivers so you can see which parts of the pipeline are driving spend and optimize or set budgets.
- To power dashboards and alerts: logged trace metrics feed monitoring dashboards (latency, error rate, LLM latency, cost graphs) and alerting rules (e.g., latency > threshold → Slack/PagerDuty), enabling fast on‑call response.
- To separate concerns from experiment tracking: tracing gives the runtime journey (input→output), while experiment tracking records pipeline configurations and versions — both are needed but serve different operational needs.
- To support post‑deployment health and safety: observability lets you monitor user journeys, infra health, and detect anomalies that could indicate malicious inputs, guardrail bypasses, or system failures.

In short: observability (tracing + monitoring) is required to debug correctness, find performance bottlenecks, attribute cost, and automate alerts for RAG pipelines in production.
- **Expected Output:** Observability is needed to open the RAG “black box” and trace what happens to an input end to end: user input, retriever output, context building, prompt augmentation, and LLM response. It also helps track latency and token usage, so you can see where time and cost are going and improve the pipeline before sending it to users.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_28

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Why add guardrails after evals/observability instead of earlier in the LLM pipeline?
- **Actual Output:** - Because evaluation is for testing and iterating. You run many tests and improve the app in multiple iterations; if you add guardrails early you’ll end up re-implementing or changing them each iteration. It’s more efficient to finalize behavior first, then put guardrails around the final iteration.

- Because observability requires opening the “black box” (tracing input→output). Guardrails are like a gift wrap — they don’t change the internal opening strategy but add an extra layer you’d have to remove to see internals. Applying them before tracing gives no benefit and only complicates debugging/inspection.

- Guardrails are application-level, not just LLM-level. You need to observe real app behavior (retrieval, embeddings, rerankers, sensitive-doc leaks, etc.) to know what application-specific rules are required (e.g., block PII, competitor checks, course-only answers). So you design guardrails after evaluation/observability when you understand failure modes.

In short: evaluate and observe to discover and iterate on real behavior first; then add guardrails as the final boundary layer to avoid rework and ensure the rules match the actual risks.
- **Expected Output:** Because evals and observability help you first find what is actually wrong in the pipeline and which version works best. After that, guardrails are added on the final selected version to set the right boundaries before production. If you add them too early, you may keep changing them again and again during iteration.

</details>

Great recall. |

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_29

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do observability and traceability differ, and why are both needed for debugging?
- **Actual Output:** - What they are
  - Traceability: tracking the journey of a specific input from end to end — e.g., user query → retriever (which documents retrieved) → how context was built and augmented into the prompt → LLM call → LLM response. It’s like putting a GPS tracker on that input so you can follow each handoff.
  - Observability: the broader capability that “opens the black box.” It collects traces, logs and metrics (token usage, latency, errors, cost, model versions, infra health) across the system so you can see behavior and trends, both during development and after deployment.

- How they differ
  - Traceability is narrow and event-centered: it answers “what happened to this input and which component produced which output.”
  - Observability is system-centered: it gives metrics and signals that explain system state and behavior (performance, cost, failures, spikes) and lets you drill into traces like the above.

- Why you need both for debugging
  - Traceability tells you where the failure occurred (which component returned bad docs, or truncated context, or malformed prompt).
  - Observability tells you why it occurred (model version changed, token limits hit, latency/timeouts, resource exhaustion, configuration drift, cost spikes).
  - Together you can reproduce the issue (experiment tracking/parameters), fix the root cause, and verify fixes — both during developer QA and continuously after deployment.

- Quick example
  - A user question gets a wrong answer. Traceability shows the retriever returned irrelevant docs. Observability shows a recent retriever config change or vector-store indexing errors or higher latency causing truncated context. With both, you find the faulty component and the underlying cause, fix it, and measure impact.
- **Expected Output:** Observability is the broader ability to open the “black box” and see what’s happening inside your RAG/LLM pipeline. Traceability is the specific tracking of an input through each step, like: input → retriever → context building → prompt → LLM response.

Both are needed for debugging because observability helps you inspect the whole system, while traceability helps you pinpoint exactly where something went wrong and how the input was handled at each stage.

</details>

## ✅ PASS - test_case_30

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do offline evals differ from online evals in timing, data, and bugs caught?
- **Actual Output:** - Timing:
  - Offline eval: runs before deployment (pre-release), typically once per test run and used as a gate in CI/CD.
  - Online eval: runs after deployment on live system, continuously or regularly on production traffic.

- Data:
  - Offline eval: uses a fixed, created golden dataset with an answer key (known correct answers).
  - Online eval: uses live production traffic with no golden dataset or answer key; you must estimate/measure on the fly (often via sampling).

- Bugs/risks caught:
  - Offline eval: primarily catches correctness issues and regressions (used for gating and version comparison).
  - Online eval: catches drift and emergent production problems (e.g., bias, increased latency under load, surprises) and monitors whether the application is running normally.
- **Expected Output:** Offline evals are run **before deployment** on a **fixed golden dataset** with known answers, and they mainly catch **regressions**.  
Online evals run **after deployment** on **live production traffic** without an answer key, and they catch **drift, surprise inputs, and emergent bugs/failures**.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_31

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do offline evals enable pre-release gating, version comparison, and regression detection?
- **Actual Output:** - Pre-release gating
  - Because offline eval uses a fixed golden dataset and answer key, you can compute objective scores for a candidate build before deployment.
  - Integrate the eval into CI/CD: run the eval automatically on every change, and only allow deployment if the score exceeds a preset threshold (example from the context: deploy only if score > 95%). If the threshold fails, block or roll back the release.

- Version comparison
  - Run the same offline eval (same golden dataset, same metrics) against different versions/configurations so the comparison is on a level field.
  - This lets you decide between alternatives (example: Clot model vs OpenAI model, different prompts, re-rankers, vector DBs) by directly comparing their scores on identical test inputs.

- Regression detection
  - Because offline evals are fast, cheap, repeatable and run on a stable golden set, you can re-run them after code or model changes to detect drops in performance relative to a baseline.
  - Any unexpected score decrease flags a regression before release, allowing you to fix it rather than shipping broken behavior.

Why this works: the golden dataset + answer key gives a measurable notion of correctness, and repeatable runs let you automate gates, compare variants, and catch regressions. (Note: offline evals do not cover production-only risks like drift or emergent user queries—those require online evals.)
- **Expected Output:** Offline evals help in three ways:

1. **Pre-release gating**: You test the software before deployment, and if the eval score meets the threshold, you release it; otherwise, you block or roll back the release.  
2. **Version comparison**: You run the same eval on two versions of the system to compare models, prompts, vector databases, or architectures fairly.  
3. **Regression detection**: After a change, you check that the improved part got better without causing other parts to get worse. If performance drops on some cases, that’s regression.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_32

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do offline evals differ from online evals after deployment?
- **Actual Output:** - Timing: offline evals run before deployment (pre-release); online evals run after deployment on live production traffic.

- Data: offline uses a fixed, curated golden dataset you create and control; online works on unpredictable, real user traffic (no fixed dataset).

- Answer key: offline usually has a ground-truth answer key (golden answers); online has no answer key and must estimate or infer correctness on the fly.

- Purpose: offline checks correctness of the application against expected answers (used for gating); online checks whether the application is running normally in production (drift, emergent failures, user-facing issues).

- What they detect: offline catches regressions relative to the golden dataset and supports version comparisons; online detects drift, emergent systematic failures (biases, latency under load, surprising user behavior) and production surprises.

- Role in deployment: offline evals can act as CI/CD release gates (automatic deploy if score > threshold); online evals monitor health and trigger alerts, rollbacks, or further investigation after release.

- Cost and speed: offline is fast, cheap and repeatable (small, controlled runs); online can be costly and slower at scale, so sampling is often used rather than evaluating every interaction.

- Inputs/coverage: offline evaluates anticipated scenarios you included; online must handle anything users ask and cover unanticipated cases.

- Comparisons and experiments: offline is ideal for controlled A/B comparisons (models, prompts, components); online evaluates real-world impact and behavior under real user distributions.

- Complementarity: they are not replacements—both are needed. Offline verifies correctness before release; online ensures normal operation and safety in production.

Example (UPSC grader): offline evaluation checks whether model scores match human marks (correctness). After deployment, online eval cannot measure that same correctness directly (no golden answers); instead it monitors if the grader is behaving normally and detects drift or unexpected errors.
- **Expected Output:** Offline evals are done **before deployment** using a **fixed golden dataset with known answers**. They are mainly used for **pre-release testing, comparing versions, and catching regression**.

Online evals happen **after deployment** on **live production traffic**. They work **without a golden answer key** and are used to catch **production risks like drift, unexpected inputs, bias, latency issues, and other emergent failures**.

</details>

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_33

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do MAE and human-in-loop evals differ in judging LLM outputs?
- **Actual Output:** MAE = machine/LLM-as-a-judge (LLM-as-judge) vs human-in-the-loop — key differences based on the given context:

- Who judges
  - MAE: an LLM (configured with a persona/system prompt) compares application output to an expected/golden answer and scores/explains.
  - Human-in-loop: one or more humans inspect outputs, apply rubrics, or step in for hard/ambiguous cases.

- Setup & inputs
  - MAE: judge is given a system prompt (persona, court/task), the application output, the expected output, and explicit scoring criteria (clarity, coverage, factuality, etc.).
  - Human-in-loop: humans may create rubrics, golden datasets, perform red‑teaming, AB testing, or directly rate outputs.

- Scalability & speed
  - MAE: highly scalable and fast (call many APIs in parallel); token cost is a factor but generally scales better than human time.
  - Human-in-loop: low daily throughput, needs rest/pay, can’t scale easily.

- Consistency & repeatability
  - MAE: repeatable and consistent to the extent the judge model and prompt are fixed; works better for non‑word‑for‑word, probabilistic output comparisons.
  - Human-in-loop: can be inconsistent (bias, sickness, fatigue); multiple human graders are often used to expose rubric ambiguity or increase reliability.

- Cost tradeoffs
  - MAE: token/API cost; judge should be a stronger/larger model than the evaluated model.
  - Human-in-loop: salary/time costs; may be “cheaper per analysis” in some cases but not scalable.

- Handling ambiguity and safety
  - MAE: can evaluate probabilistic/non-deterministic outputs where programmatic exact-match fails; “poison cuts poison” drawback (using LLMs to judge LLMs has limitations).
  - Human-in-loop: better for complex, safety-critical, or grey-area decisions (red‑teaming, final judgments, creating/validating rubrics and golden data).

- Best practice from context
  - Use MAE for large-scale, repeatable scoring when a stronger judge model and clear prompt/rubric exist.
  - Use humans for red‑teaming, rubric creation, golden datasets, AB testing, and as a fallback for ambiguous or high-risk cases (human-in-the-loop).

In short: MAE trades human time and variability for scalable, prompt-driven LLM consistency (with token cost and model-quality caveats), while human-in-loop provides nuanced judgment, safety oversight, and rubric creation but at the cost of scalability and consistency.
- **Expected Output:** MAE is a metric that compares LLM scores with human scores and measures the average deviation between them; the goal is to get this error as close to zero as possible. Human-in-the-loop evals, on the other hand, involve humans directly judging outputs in complex cases where automated evaluation is not enough.

</details>

Since there are no irrelevant nodes above any relevant node, nothing needs to be ranked lower. |

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_34

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** Why do eval tasks need multiple pipelines, and which 4 human eval methods support them?
- **Actual Output:** Why multiple pipelines:
- One single eval cannot capture all failure modes or application needs. Different apps (RAG, agents, conversational systems) require different checks (relevance, recall/precision/reranking, hallucination, safety, multi‑turn behavior, business alignment, etc.).  
- Multiple pipelines let you detect ambiguous rubrics, measure different metrics, compare component changes (e.g., chunk size 500 vs 501), and cover security/application‑level guardrails.  
- In short: diversity of use cases and possible failures → need multiple, specialized eval pipelines.

Four human eval methods that support these pipelines:
1. Grading / rating (human scorers evaluate outputs against rubrics).  
2. Red teaming (human attackers probe for failure modes and safety breaks).  
3. A/B testing (real users compare versions in production and rate experience).  
4. Golden dataset creation (humans build gold labels and rubrics used for automated and manual checks).

(Also commonly used: human‑in‑the‑loop for complex/gray cases where programmatic/LLM checks are insufficient.)
- **Expected Output:** Eval tasks need multiple pipelines because one evaluation method cannot capture all failure modes of an LLM application; different apps like RAG, agents, and chatbots need different metrics and checks. The 4 human eval methods mentioned are: grading/rating answers, red teaming, A/B testing, and creating golden datasets/rubrics.

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_35

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How do human evals use red-teaming and A/B testing for LLM assessment?
- **Actual Output:** Red teaming
- A dedicated group of humans intentionally tries to “attack” the LLM system to find where it breaks (safety failures, misuse, edge-case failures).
- Whenever the system breaks, the failing prompts, responses and context are collected and sent back to the development team so the model/system can be fixed.
- This is used especially when large models are launched to surface dangerous capabilities or weaknesses that automated checks or simple grading might miss.
- It is a human-driven, adversarial form of evaluation distinct from routine grading/rating.

A/B testing
- Two versions of the chatbot/system are put into production simultaneously.
- Real users interact with each version and are asked (or instrumented) to rate their experience or the system’s outputs.
- Compare the user ratings/metrics; the version with better measured experience is selected and rolled out more widely.
- This gives production feedback on actual user preference and real-world performance, and complements offline grading and automated checks.

How they complement other human evals
- Both are different from single-rater grading, golden-dataset labeling, or ad-hoc human-in-the-loop judgments: red teaming finds adversarial/safety failures, A/B testing measures live user experience, while grading/golden sets measure correctness against defined criteria.
- Together they help discover ambiguous rubric issues, dangerous behaviors, and real-world regressions that simple scoring or programmatic checks may miss.
- **Expected Output:** Human evals use **red-teaming** to intentionally stress-test the LLM and find where it breaks, then send those failure cases back to improve the system. They use **A/B testing** by comparing two chatbot versions in production and collecting user ratings or feedback to choose the better one.

</details>

- There will still be a need for at least one knowledgeable person in every company to design, judge and run systems.

- Building base models may decline as a common job; optimizing, fine-tuning, securing, auditing and operationalizing AI will be in demand.
- Upskilling, focusing on core strengths, and cultivating curiosity (not fear) are recommended to avoid being replaceable.

In short: the title may persist but responsibilities will evolve toward higher-level engineering, review, safety, and AI-ops work rather than just typing code.
- **Expected Output:** Yes, software engineering will still exist in 5 years.

AI may generate thousands of lines of code quickly, but the bigger need will be code review, judging whether the code is meaningful, useful, and scalable, and understanding the engineering side of the system. So the field will evolve, not disappear — and people with strong understanding of engineering and AI will be even more valuable.

## ❌ FAIL - test_case_38

<details><summary><b>View Test Case Data</b></summary>

- **Input:** How can a mentor remove fear, spark curiosity, and set clear, measurable goals?
- **Actual Output:** Remove fear
- Normalize the feeling: acknowledge that switching domains or learning new skills is hard (as you heard: “very common doubt”). Share real stories of people who changed fields — bring alumni/experts who did it so learners see it’s possible.
- Start small and safe: give tiny, low-stakes tasks (short videos, micro-projects, reading a playlist) so learners get early wins and build confidence.
- Provide predictable support: a clear route for doubts (a support form, a group/WhatsApp channel, scheduled doubt sessions) so learners know help is available rather than feeling abandoned.
- Create a psychological safety net: praise effort, show common failure points, and model how to recover (e.g., iterate on a failed mini-project).

Spark curiosity
- Show meaningful, concrete applications: demo short projects that solve real problems (even business scenarios) so learners see what they could build.
- Offer a curated path: share roadmaps/playlists (like the suggested DSMP/ML playlists) and explain “why this follows that” so learners understand the logic behind topics.
- Invite practitioners: run Q&A or panel sessions with people who transitioned into AI; live interaction exposes learners to different journeys and questions.
- Encourage exploration loops: assign mini-research tasks—read one paper, try one tutorial, then discuss findings in a group—to build inquiry habits.

Set clear, measurable goals
- Start with one target: ask the learner to choose a single, concrete outcome (e.g., “build and present an end-to-end NLP project,” “get an AI internship,” or “complete LangChain mini‑project”).
- Break it into milestones with timelines: divide the target into 4–8 week milestones (course modules, mini-projects, interview prep) and give dates.
- Define objective metrics for each milestone:
  - Learning metrics: finish X videos, complete Y exercises, score Z on a mock test.
  - Output metrics: complete project A with README and demo video, publish code on GitHub, prepare 3 interview case answers.
  - Assessment metrics: attend a mock interview, get feedback from an expert, or pass a rubric that mixes easy/medium/hard items (like a 15-question exam covering full syllabus).
- Use regular checkpoints: schedule group reviews or expert sessions to evaluate progress, give feedback, and re-calibrate goals.
- Make goals visible and accountable: track progress in a shared place (WhatsApp group, spreadsheet, or form) so mentor and peer group can support and hold accountable.

Practical, scalable setup (given constraints mentioned)
- Use group mentorship and expert drop-ins instead of one-on-one. Bring people who’ve transitioned for structured sessions.
- Use forms for doubt submission and periodic curated doubt-clearing sessions.
- Provide templates: goal-setting template, milestone checklist, project rubric (cover breadth + mixed difficulty) so students can self-evaluate and mentors can give focused feedback.

Putting it together: empathize to remove fear, feed curiosity with real projects and experts, and convert aspirations into a single target broken into measurable milestones with periodic expert checks and scalable support channels.
- **Expected Output:** A mentor can do this by:

- **Removing fear:** reassure the learner that making mistakes is part of growth and that they don’t need to know everything right away.
- **Sparking curiosity:** encourage questions, exploration, and a mindset of “what can I learn next?” instead of panic about AI or change.
- **Setting clear, measurable goals:** focus on one target role or skill path, then break it into specific steps and track progress instead of trying to learn everything.

In short: **less fear, more curiosity, and a focused plan with measurable milestones.**

</details>
